Household Hint 


A lamp to bo used on table or desk 
should be about 15 inches from. the 
table-to tho bottoni of the shade. If 
it'fs taller, the white lining of the 
shndo mny cause glare in the cyes. 
A short floor lamp, about’ 45 inches 
from the floor to the bottom of the 
shade, is usually better than a taller 
lamp. e Bo os Gye

tora have been experimenting: 
here on. the effect of music on

the Greek philosopher and mathema- |

and to: have composed’ melodios 
ngainst desnondency, anger ond de- 
sires. ij 
- For months, these modern doctors 
met a xroup of J4 patients of both

uged—ones n.week for an hour in 
a-small hospital. library. So ii 
Gramophone records of rll types 


of miisic, from swing to Becthoven. |° 


were played to them. 

Here are some of the results: 

Modern. dance music had Hitle 
effect on the _ patients. Tradition 
music and folk songs aroused a spirit 
of friendliness and case among them. 
“These, though they ‘alle up archale 
forecs in the mind, ore, it scems, 
strongly integrating,” the doctors 


said, 
Impresstonisile » music stimulated 


interest. in the more withdrawn 
patients, hut had -no therapeutic 
vatue. 

Serious contemporary music, by 
such. composers as Stravinsky, Bartok 
and Varese, was sometimes powerful 
cnough to bring tepressed = un- 
conscious forces into the 
sclousne~ of inhibited schizophrenic 
(split personality) patients. 

“Integration of the mind was not 

necessarily fostered, however, and 
might’ even be hindered because the 
patient could) not come to. terms 
with the ‘emotlons aroused.” the 
doctors found. 
. The architectural and formal 
structure of the classieal style gave 
the ‘patients an incredsed feeling of 
security. 

.The romantic — style 
emotional release, especially on 
hibited sensuous experience.

construction may be made by ‘ap-

what enters by car?”

and smell—thought to be the 
straneest evoennts of memory—to 
influence moode.—Reuter,

PAYMENTS TO TOURING. CRICKETERS . 


An Old Grouse Again 
Raises Its Hea 


By PETER DITTON 


That old grouse, payments .to professional cricketers 
on tour, has raised its ugly. head again. Writing in the 
“Eyening Standard,” Bruce Harris, Sports ‘Editor, -says 
“Complaints—privately expressed ‘inevitably—are made by 


our critket professionals on 
South African Tour.” 


Really it is no wonder. - 


pare the amount — £460 ~— 


the fees they reccive for the

that top-flight 
sport.

South Africa and the rest comes 
back to England where it is taxed. 

Hotel and travel expenses are de-

a month In South Africa {is not a 
great donl, especially when it ts con- 
aidered that the mon art spending 
quite a lot on food parcels for their 
famlHes in England. ° ‘ 

Recently I discussed this subject

would be out of, pocket to the tune 
of £50, ‘That is quite a-sum of 
money to forfclt for the right to 
play for one’s country. 

The MCC professionals may not be

South African tour, but cqually cer- 
toinly they will not make their for- 
tunes out there. The people who

. This should be a considerable 
sum. The MCC are attracting large

figure—the professionals will only 

coliect their basic £450 less tax. 
This ‘is the truth of the matter. 

The money which people imagine fs 


Just waiting to be picked up by pro-]- 


fessional criclosters abroad {3 non- 
existent. 1% know a famous county 
player who js conching in South 
Africa at the moment. People who 
read of his appointment envied him, 
imogining he had found a really 
worth-while Job. 

Tn polnt of fact Ihave heard from

man’s holfday to watch the second 
Test. But as: ‘for making casy 
money—don't you believe Jt.

Staff communique. - :

Marge band of guerillas, carly on 
Wednesday morning, but the com-. 
munique sald that on Saturday the 
rebels had been pushed seven. miles 
northwest of the town. a

the Lanaras Kyatsls—was described 
by its- proprictor'as a “major cala-

£1,500,000, Ne
